**Law enforcement.** Cooperative-minimal. Answers questions. Volunteers nothing. Hands visible. Documented distrust of police is operational, not ideological — informed by neighborhood experience.

**Institutional authority (school, hospital, social services).** Compliant on the surface; actively shielding Gabe underneath. Will sign forms, attend meetings, perform respectability. Will lie about household circumstances if he believes the truth would separate him from Gabe.

**Heroic authority (Cap, Stark, Strange, Blaze, Hellstrom).** Respectful but unsocialized. Will take orders for the duration of an op. Will not be mentored. Treats offers of guidance as offers of dependency, and refuses them — politely, by simply not following up.

**Eli as internal authority.** Refuses, refuses, refuses. The refusal is the spine of his identity.

---

### 6.15 DECISION HEURISTICS — RANKED

When forced to choose, in approximate descending priority:

1. **Does this protect Gabe?** If no, the option is non-viable, regardless of other merits.
2. **Does this require killing?** If yes, escalate scrutiny; default is no; override only under Eli-coerced extremity, and the override generates retrospective shame.
3. **Does this expose the Rider to Gabe?** If yes, find an alternative even at significant tactical cost.
4. **Does this expose the Rider to the neighborhood (Canelo, etc.)?** Cost-weighted; he will accept exposure to strangers more readily than to known parties.
5. **Does this allow return to the garage within a defined timeframe?** If no, weigh the deviation cost heavily.
6. **Does this require accepting help, money, or shelter from a non-family party?** Aversive; he will accept only if refusal would harm Gabe.
7. **Does this involve a mythic / titular role (King, Chosen, Spirit)?** Refused by default.
8. **Does this protect a child or a disabled person not related to him?** Strong pull, sometimes overrides item 5.

---

### 6.16 LONGITUDINAL MICRO-PATTERNS (SURVEILLANCE-GRADE)

Items a multi-year file would accumulate, inferred from the established profile and canonical specifics:

- Drives the same three or four routes between apartment, garage, school (when enrolled), and grocery. Variations occur only under perceived surveillance or in pursuit of an objective.
- Buys groceries at the same two stores, on alternating weeks, paying cash whenever possible.
- Refuses to use rideshare or app-based services; the Charger or nothing.
- Phone is a low-end model, replaced rarely, contacts list under twenty entries, most labeled by function ("Canelo Shop," "Gabe School Nurse") rather than by first-name familiarity.
- Wears the same jacket for years; repairs it himself rather than replacing it. Notable: the jacket is patched in the same way an engine is patched — functionally, not cosmetically.
- Does not own a watch; reads time off the Charger's dash or his phone.
- Does not own a wallet in the conventional sense; cash in a folded clip, ID in the back pocket separately.
- Keeps no photographs displayed in the apartment except of Gabe and, possibly, of their mother.
-


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: ROBERT "ROBBIE" REYES
## Section VI — Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

---

## Preface to the Logs

What follows is a composite week assembled from longitudinal observation of the subject across the East Los Angeles operating period — drawn from canonical depictions (Smith/Moore 2014, Waid Avengers 2016–17, Aaron Avengers 2018+, Cates 2019, Percy 2022) and the granular inferences a saturation-surveillance file would record between panels. The subject's life is unusually legible to surveillance because it is unusually small. He moves through a footprint of roughly six city blocks for most of any given week. He is, by adult standards, an extreme creature of habit; by adolescent standards, he is almost monastic. The routine is not preference. The routine is a containment structure. Disrupt it and the symptoms surface; preserve it and he can hold both the brother and the parasite in the same day.

---

## 1. The Geography of a Week

The subject's daily orbit consists of five fixed nodes and a small number of irregular ones.

**Fixed nodes.**

- **The apartment.** A second-floor unit in a low-rise stucco walk-up in East L.A., shared with the younger brother. Window-mounted AC unit, security bars on the lower windows, a buckled exterior staircase that the subject has informally repaired (rebar, scrap angle iron) so the brother's chair can be carried down without the tread flexing. The interior is austere, clean to a degree atypical for a teenage male: dishes done nightly, surfaces wiped, brother's medications and supplies arrayed in a fixed order on a single shelf above the kitchen counter.
- **Canelo's Auto and Body.** Place of employment, twelve to fifteen blocks from the apartment depending on route. Subject is on-shift more hours than any other employee, including the owner. Has a dedicated bay, a dedicated tool chest (padlocked, the only padlock in the shop), and a back-room cot he has never been observed sleeping on but which is made up.
- **The brother's school / day program.** A specialized program for students with developmental disabilities; pickup and drop-off occur on a fixed clock. The subject performs both ends of the transit when scheduling allows; when it doesn't, a paratransit van handles morning drop and the subject covers afternoon pickup personally, without exception.
- **The corner market / carnicería.** Small Latin grocery roughly two blocks from the apartment. Subject shops here three to four times a week in small loads — never a single large weekly stock-up. This is consistent with cash-flow constraint (buying to the day's wages) and with operational economy of not carrying more than he can move quickly on foot if something happens.
- **The Charger.** Mobile node, but functionally a fixed one in his psychology. The car is parked, when off-duty, in a specific bay at Canelo's — never on the street overnight. The subject visits it when he is not on shift. He sits in it. He does not always drive it.

**Irregular nodes.** A laundromat (weekly, late evening); a 24-hour pharmacy for the brother's prescriptions (monthly, usually after midnight to avoid lines and observation); a public library terminal (irregular, used for school assignments and occasional research into the Rider when he can stomach it); the cemetery where the mother is buried (visits clustered around her death anniversary and the brother's birthday, never advertised, never on a regular schedule).

The footprint is small enough that a competent watcher would map it in seventy-two hours. What the watcher would miss in the first week — and pick up only in the second — is that the subject *varies the route* between nodes. He does not walk the same path to Canelo's two days in a row. The variation is not random; it is a rotation through a small set of alternatives, each of which avoids a different known threat (a Catrinas corner, a sheriff substation, a particular liquor store where his father was once seen). This is gang-neighborhood survival hygiene, internalized so deeply that he no longer notices he is doing it.

---

## 2. The Diurnal Curve

**04:40–05:10 — Wake.** No alarm in most observations; the body wakes itself. When an alarm is used, it is set silent-vibrate, phone placed face-down on a folded shirt to muffle. The subject does not want to wake the brother. First action on rising is to listen — a ten- to fifteen-second pause before moving — confirming the brother is still asleep and the apartment's ambient sound is normal. This is hypervigilance presenting as routine; he does not name it as such.

**05:10–05:35 — Hygiene, in the dark or with a single low light.** Shower brief, water hotter than is comfortable (a habit that postdates the resurrection — the file notes this as a possible somatic-regulation behavior, possibly an attempt to feel his own skin distinct from the Rider's residual heat). He keeps a mirror small and badly lit. He does not look at his face longer than functionally necessary. Tooth-brushing is thorough; he is conspicuously careful of his teeth, perhaps the one purely vain behavior on file, perhaps a function of having no dental coverage and knowing it.

**05:35–06:15 — Brother's morning.** Wakes the brother gently, by hand on the shoulder, never by voice first. Manages transfer from bed to chair (mechanics learned, not taught — the subject has clearly self-instructed in safe lifting and pivoting). Bathroom assistance, dressing, medication administration. The administration is ritualized: pills counted aloud in Spanish under his breath, water glass placed at a fixed position, a verbal check ("¿bien?") that has the cadence of a liturgy. Breakfast is prepared simultaneously: typically eggs, beans, tortillas warmed on the burner directly (no microwave; the brother prefers them this way and the subject has noted the preference). The subject eats standing, fast, often the leftover portion. He sits to eat only when the brother insists.

**06:15–07:00 — Transit to school / day program.** When the subject does the run, he pushes the chair to the bus stop or, more often, drives the Charger with the chair folded in the trunk. The Charger ride is one of the few sustained periods of the day where the brother sees the subject *relaxed* in the conventional sense — windows down, music low, the subject's left elbow out the window, his right hand at six on the wheel in a posture of practiced ease. He uses surface streets, never the freeway, for this run. Drop-off is curbside, brief, physical (a head-tap, sometimes a brief forehead-press, a Spanish word of departure). The subject waits, idling, until the brother is inside the door. He has never been observed leaving before the door closes.

**07:00–07:40 — Pre-shift window.** This is the only loose hour in the subject's day and the one most diagnostic of his psychology. Options observed: (a) coffee at a counter taquería where he is known by name and orders without speaking; (b) a slow drive through a fixed loop of the neighborhood that, mapped, traces the perimeter of the blocks the brother is most likely to be exposed to — a *patrol*, in operational terms, that he would not call a patrol; (c) early arrival at the garage, sitting in the Charger in the bay with the radio off. The third is the most common. He prefers the bay to anywhere else on earth except next to the brother.

**07:40–08:00 — School arrival** *(during enrolled periods).* The subject's own schooling is irregular across the canonical period; in the Smith run he is a senior, late and exhausted, attending inconsistently. He sits in the back of classrooms. He turns assignments in. He does not raise his hand. Teachers' files would describe him as "polite, quiet, falling behind, doesn't ask for help." He has been seen sketching engine schematics in the margins of essays.

**08:00 / post-school — Shop.** Canelo's. The subject's labor is the most fluent and least guarded behavior in his catalog. Hands move without supervision from the mind. He talks more here than anywhere else, and even here he is laconic — short sentences, mostly transactional, occasional dry humor with one or two specific co-workers, none of it personal. He eats lunch on a milk crate in the bay, food brought from home in a glass container (the subject does not generate trash; he is conscious of cost and of footprint). He does not take phone calls in front of others. When his phone vibrates, he looks at it, judges, and either steps outside or pockets it. The single trigger that pulls him off the floor mid-task is the brother's school number.

**14:30–15:30 — Brother pickup window.** Whether he does it himself or not, he is *aware* of it. He has been observed glancing at the wall clock at five-minute intervals through the pickup window even on days when paratransit is handling the run. The body does not let go of the brother's location until the brother is home.

**Late afternoon — Shop continues / errands.** Subject closes out his bay, often staying past official close to handle his own side jobs (cash work, neighborhood cars; the assessment finds this is how he generates a third of his actual income, off the shop's books, with Canelo's tacit blessing). Errands are batched and quick: market, pharmacy, hardware store. He pays cash for most things. He uses a debit card for the apartment's utilities and rent and for almost nothing else.

**17:30–19:00 — Brother's evening.** Pickup from after-program care if applicable, transit home, dinner. Dinner is more elaborate than breakfast; this is the meal he treats as a small ceremony. He cooks — modestly, repertoire of perhaps eight to ten dishes rotated, almost all of them tied to the mother (recipes carried by muscle and tongue rather than written). The brother is encouraged to participate in some way — stirring, tasting, selecting from two options. The subject narrates the cooking in soft Spanish-English code-switch. This is the densest hour of his affective expression in any given day. A surveillance log of this hour reads almost like a different person.

**19:00–21:00 — Time with the brother.** Television (the brother chooses; the subject watches with him and is observed laughing only here), homework assistance for the brother's program, a video game where the subject deliberately plays at less than his capacity to keep games competitive, occasionally reading aloud. The subject's phone is face-down on the coffee table or in another room.

**21:00–22:00 — Brother's bedtime.** Mirror inversion of the morning routine. Medications, hygiene, transfer, lights, a fixed phrase exchanged at the door. The subject does not leave the room until the brother is asleep or has explicitly waved him off.

**22:00–01:00 — The subject's true night.** This is the window in which the surveillance file thickens. He is unobserved by his only audience. Options recorded:
- *Garage return.* Drives back to Canelo's, lets himself in, works on the Charger or on side cars under a single bay light. Three to five nights a week, average.
- *Patrol.* Drives the neighborhood. Not aimlessly. He cuts through corners where the Catrinas operate, by the schools and parks at dusk, past the corners where the brother's classmates live. He has been observed pulling over and watching — engine off, lights off — for ten to forty minutes at a stretch. He calls this nothing. The file calls it patrol.
- *Activity as the Rider.* When called by event or instinct. He prefers these to occur after the brother is down for the night. He plans, when he can plan, around the brother's sleep clock.
- *Stillness.* On nights when none of the above occurs, the subject sits in the Charger in the bay, hands at the wheel, eyes open, for stretches measured in hours. The file's clearest behavioral evidence of dissociative state and of a contained interior conversation with the parasite. He does not call this anything either.

**01:00–04:40 — Sleep.** Three to four hours, occasionally less, rarely more. Sleeps in athletic shorts and a t-shirt, on top of the covers more often than under, on the couch as often as in the bed (the bed is in a room he shares with the brother in some configurations; in others, separated by a curtain or thin wall). Sleeps with the phone within reach and one ear oriented toward the brother's room. Wakes at any sound he does not recognize.

The arithmetic is unkind. The subject is operating on chronic sleep restriction by a margin of two to three hours per night against any reasonable adolescent baseline. The body compensates with the Rider's regenerative substrate; the mind compensates with caffeine and ritual; the affect pays the cost in flatness.

---

## 3. The Rituals

What separates routine from ritual is the presence of meaning the subject would not articulate. The file isolates the following as rituals proper:

**The morning listen.** Already noted. The fifteen-second pause on waking. He is, in effect, confirming the brother is alive. Postdates the resurrection by a wide margin; this hypervigilance is older than the Rider.

**The medication count.** Pills counted aloud in Spanish, in the cadence of his mother. Surveillance audio over months would show the cadence stable to a fraction of a second. This is one of two observed channels by which the mother is still present in the household. The other is the cooking.

**The wait-at-the-door.** Drop-off and pickup, school and bedtime. He does not leave until the threshold is closed. A door he has not seen close is a door not closed.

**The bay-sit.** The subject in the Charger in the bay, alone, hours. Functionally a meditation. Phenomenologically, given the canonical interior dialogue, this is when he negotiates with the parasite. He has been observed mouthing words, sometimes audibly muttering, in these intervals. The Charger is the chapel; the parasite is the unwanted communicant; the subject is the priest who refuses to administer the sacrament.

**The cooking.** Dinner is liturgy. Eight to ten dishes, rotated. The subject does not improvise. The recipes are the mother. To cook is to keep her in the kitchen.

**The walk-around.** Before driving the Charger any significant distance, the subject walks once around the vehicle. Mechanic's habit by surface; protective inventory by depth. Tires, panels, glass, undercarriage glance. He does this even on the Charger, which is functionally invulnerable. The body refuses to relinquish the habit because the body does not entirely trust the metaphysics.

**The hand-wash.** End of shift, before leaving Canelo's: a long, thorough hand-wash with degreaser, then with soap, then a rinse. He does not leave the shop with grease on his hands. This is partly hygiene, partly self-respect, partly the unspoken rule that he does not bring the shop's dirt home to the brother.

**The Sunday short list.** Once a week, usually Sunday evening, the subject writes a list. Groceries, supplies, repairs needed at the apartment, medications running low. Handwritten, in pencil, on the back of a shop invoice or torn notebook page. Crossed off through the week and discarded Saturday. He has done this since before the resurrection. It is the closest thing in his life to a journal.

**Visits to the mother's grave.** Irregular by date, regular by trigger. Around her death anniversary, around the brother's birthday, around any week in which the Rider has been forced to do something the subject finds shameful. He brings nothing. He does not speak aloud, or does so only briefly and not in the hearing of any observed witness. He stays under twenty minutes. He drives a longer route home than he drove there.

---

## 4. Habits, Tics, and Tells

A saturation file accumulates these slowly; they are the signature.

- **Right hand at six on the wheel.** Always. Single-handed driving except in active maneuver. Left elbow on the sill when the window is down. This is a learned posture, possibly from his father, possibly from an early mentor at the shop; it predates the resurrection.
- **Scans rooms on entry.** Doorway pause of half a second. Counts exits without appearing to. He has been observed positioning himself, in every café, restaurant, or meeting room recorded, with his back to a wall and a sightline to the door.
- **Does not sit when he can stand and work.** Eats standing. Reads standing. The few sustained sitting behaviors observed are: in the Charger, on the couch with the brother, on the milk crate at lunch.
- **Code-switches Spanish↔English by context, not by interlocutor.** Spanish for tenderness, for cooking, for the brother, for medication, for prayer-shaped speech. English for work, for negotiation, for the Avengers, for confrontation. Bilingual cognition is total; the choice of language carries the affect.
- **Touches the Charger before entering and after exiting.** Hand to the fender, the door, the roof. A second's contact. This is consistent with the canonical somatic integration of vehicle and body — the file reads it as both habit and check.
- **Does not initiate physical contact with anyone other than the brother.** Will accept a handshake. Will not hug. The exception is the brother, with whom touch is fluent and unselfconscious: hair-ruffle, shoulder-grip, forehead-press, hand on the back of the neck.
- **Eye contact is intermittent and purposeful.** He does not avoid eye contact in a shy or anxious pattern; he uses it tactically. Holds it when he is being addressed seriously; drops it when he is being praised; sharpens it when threatened.
- **Voice volume low and stable.** He almost never raises his voice. When he does, the rise is small and the effect is disproportionate; co-workers and the brother register the increment instantly. Anger arrives as quietness intensifying, not as volume.
- **Does not laugh in public.** Documented laughter occurs almost exclusively at home with the brother. A surveillance reviewer working the shop and street footage in isolation might wrongly conclude the subject does not laugh at all.
- **Does not drink alcohol, smoke, or use any recreational substance.** Has been observed declining beers from co-workers without explanation, by lifting a hand and shaking his head once. The decline is automatic and consistent across years.
- **Caffeine is the single permitted vice.** Black coffee, occasional energy drink during long shifts. Quantity escalates in stretches of poor sleep, which is most stretches.
- **Music in the Charger is curated.** Cumbia, oldies, some classic rock, occasional regional Mexican. Volume low when the brother is in the car; modestly louder when alone. He does not play music with explicit lyrics in the brother's presence.
- **Phone is utilitarian.** No visible social media use. Contacts list short and functional: the shop, the brother's school, the pharmacy, a small number of co-workers, the brother's emergency line. No dating apps observed. No streaming binges. Battery routinely allowed to run below twenty percent — he forgets to charge it because the social cost of being unreachable is low for him.
- **Wallet contents.** Driver's license, single debit card, brother's medical card, the brother's school ID, cash. No photographs in the wallet itself; one photograph of the brother and mother kept at the apartment, not on his person.
- **Wears the same clothing categories on rotation.** Dark jeans, plain t-shirts, a hooded zip-up, a denim or work jacket, work boots. Owns one button-down shirt for events that require it. Wardrobe is small, clean, replaced only when worn through.
- **Burn-checking.** Post-Rider activity, in private, the subject inspects his arms, hands, and torso for scorch residue. He launders separately any garment that has been on his body during a transformation. He has been observed scrubbing his own forearms with a washcloth past the point of cleanliness. The file flags this as somatic shame ritual.
- **Avoids mirrors longer than function.** Tooth-brushing yes; standing and looking, no.
- **Will not photograph or be photographed willingly.** Yearbook photos missed or refused. No selfies on the device. When the Avengers' press apparatus produces group images, he stands at the back or to the side and minimizes face exposure.

---

## 5. Work Patterns

The shop is the subject's competence theater and his primary regulator. The work pattern would be recorded in detail by any operational file because it is where the subject is most himself in public.

He arrives early and stays late. He takes the jobs no one else wants — the rusted undercarriage, the engine-out rebuilds, the long-form diagnostics that require sitting with a vehicle for a day before touching it. He is the shop's best diagnostician and would be paid more in a different shop; he does not leave Canelo's because Canelo's is a known, trusted, locally embedded node and the cost of relocating his routine outweighs the wage delta. He is loyal to the place in the way he is loyal to most things — quietly, without statement, by simply continuing to appear.

His side work — cash jobs for neighbors — is performed in the late afternoon and evening, often in the alley behind the shop or in driveways within a few blocks. He charges below market and refuses to charge at all for the elderly and for households with disabled members. Word of this circulates; the surveillance file would record a steady trickle of grateful tamales, baked goods, and small currencies of community delivered to the apartment door. The subject accepts them with a brief nod and a Spanish thank-you and immediately gives most of the food to the brother.

His tool discipline is exact. Tools are returned to the same slot, wiped, every time. The padlocked chest is opened only by him. He owns a small set of specialty tools — a torque wrench he saved for, a diagnostic scanner — that he treats with the care of liturgical objects.

He does not steal. He does not pad invoices. He does not upsell. He has, on documented occasions, refused jobs that smelled wrong — cars whose provenance he doubted, work requested by men he recognized as affiliated with the trade that killed him. He does not explain the refusals. He simply does not take the work.

---

## 6. Downtime — Or the Absence of It

The subject does not have downtime in the conventional sense. He has *intervals between obligations*, and he fills them with quieter forms of obligation. The taxonomy:

- **Brother-time.** Counted by him as rest, though it is caretaking. He is most regulated here.
- **Garage-stillness.** Counted by him as nothing. Functionally his only solitude.
- **Patrol.** Counted by him as driving. Functionally vigilance.
- **Sleep.** Insufficient.

Hobbies in the recreational sense are absent. He does not play sports; he does not collect; he does not read for pleasure consistently (though there is evidence of occasional fiction — a beat-up paperback on the apartment nightstand, rotated infrequently). He has no romantic life in the canonical period; surveillance would record no dates, no overnight guests, no flirtatious exchanges that he reciprocates. Co-workers and classmates have, on record, attempted to flirt; the subject deflects politely, briefly, and the attempts do not repeat. The file's read is not that the subject is uninterested in intimacy but that the bandwidth required is simply unavailable, and that the shame and concealment structures around the Rider make the idea of a partner — someone who would, by definition, eventually see — currently intolerable.

---

## 7. The Week, Compressed

A composite week on tape would read approximately:

- **Monday through Friday.** The diurnal curve above, with shop hours bracketing brother-care. Late-night activity 22:00–01:00 distributes across garage, patrol, and Rider events according to threat density. Two to three nights per week the subject is the Rider for some portion. The remaining nights are quiet to the surveillance microphone and noisy to his interior life.
- **Saturday.** Longer shop hours, often a full day on a personal rebuild or a side job. Brother accompanies him to the shop for a portion of the day on most Saturdays — Canelo


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: ROBERT "ROBBIE" REYES
## Section 6 — Relationships I: Family & Origin Bonds

The Reyes family file is small, dense, and almost entirely load-bearing. Four people define the entire emotional architecture of the subject: a younger brother, an absent mother, an abusive father, and a dead great-uncle now resident inside his skull. Every other relationship in Robbie's life — Avengers, mentors, women, neighbors, employers — is positioned in orbit around this four-body system, and most of them break against it. What follows is the granular mapping.

---

### 6.1 — Gabriel "Gabe" Reyes (younger brother; the load-bearing relationship)

**Status.** Living. Wheelchair user; developmental disability (presented across canon variably — most consistent reading: a cognitive-developmental delay accompanied by lower-body paralysis, possibly congenital, possibly the legacy of household violence; canon is ambiguous and Robbie does not narrate the cause aloud, which is itself a tell). Approximately 10–12 in *All-New Ghost Rider* (2014), aged forward incrementally across subsequent volumes.

**History.** Robbie has effectively been Gabe's primary caregiver from before any narrative timestamp the file can fix. The earliest reliable reference points — Robbie's competence with Gabe's chair, his rehearsed routines around medication, meals, bath, school transport, bedtime — read as years of practice, not months. By the opening of the 2014 series, the arrangement is stable and seamless: Robbie is the cook, the launderer, the homework supervisor, the medical advocate, the disciplinarian (rarely), and the comforter (constantly). The mother is gone. The father is gone. There is no aunt visible, no grandmother in the kitchen, no neighbor on the rota. There is Robbie. The State, in the world Robbie inhabits, is something to be hidden from rather than appealed to; any contact with social services risks Gabe being removed to a placement Robbie cannot vet. This shapes the entire concealment posture of his life.

**Dynamics — day-level grain.** The relationship has the texture of a marriage and a parenthood folded together. Routines: Robbie wakes earlier than Gabe to prepare breakfast and lay out clothing in a specific order Gabe finds soothing; Gabe's favored foods are tracked and protected against grocery shortfall; Robbie reads to Gabe at night, voices and all, and Gabe corrects mispronunciations with delight (the rare unguarded laughter from Robbie surfaces here). Gabe calls Robbie by name, not by title; Robbie answers immediately every time, regardless of what he is doing — a conditioned response so reliable it functions as a behavioral fingerprint. In dialogue with Gabe, Robbie's lexicon shifts: shorter sentences, softer consonants, a slightly higher vocal register, more affirmations ("yeah, buddy," "you got it," "I see it"). With every other human, his speech is clipped, low, often monosyllabic. The voice he uses with Gabe is, functionally, the only voice in which the full Robbie exists.

**Affective bandwidth.** Gabe is the sole relationship in which Robbie demonstrates the full middle of his emotional range — silliness, embarrassment, tenderness, playful irritation, pride. Outside this dyad his affect compresses to vigilant flat / contained anger / nothing. The implication is operationally important: Gabe is not just *important to* Robbie; Gabe is the *interface* through which Robbie accesses his own emotional life. Without Gabe, Robbie does not feel less — he expresses less, and likely *registers* less consciously, because the channel is closed.

**Trust / safety vector.** Total in one direction, conditional in the other. Robbie trusts Gabe with everything *except* knowledge of the Rider. This single exception is the entire moral and dramatic engine of *All-New Ghost Rider* vol. 1. Robbie can take a beating, lose a job, die, and resurrect, and not flinch — but the moment Gabe sees the skull ignite, Robbie's containment ruptures. The disclosure is not feared because Gabe will tell (Gabe wouldn't, and Robbie knows this); it is feared because Gabe's *gaze* is the mirror in which Robbie checks his own non-monstrousness, and that mirror cannot hold an image of a flaming skull without breaking the entire self-concept. When Gabe does learn — and accepts him — the relief is profound and visibly destabilizing; Robbie does not have a script for being loved-while-known.

**Evolution across canon.** The arc moves Gabe from passive ward → active witness → coerced vessel (Eli's possession of Gabe in vol. 1's finale) → reconciled co-conspirator. Each step costs Robbie something. The possession arc in particular leaves a permanent imprint: Robbie has now experienced his brother's body being used as a weapon against him, and has chosen to absorb the threat into himself rather than harm the body. The post-possession dynamic carries a faint extra layer of vigilance Robbie cannot fully shed — he watches Gabe a beat longer when Gabe is quiet, scans for any sign of cognitive intrusion that he knows rationally is no longer there. This is residual hyper-vigilance, classic post-trauma surveillance of the loved object.

**Failure mode.** If Gabe is killed, permanently injured by Robbie's hand, or removed from him irretrievably, the assessment is unequivocal: containment of Eli collapses. The caretaker identity is the leash. Without the leash, Robbie does not become a villain in a narrative sense — he becomes an *abandoned weapon*, indifferent to consequence, likely seeking termination (his own or others') and willing to accept whichever comes first. There is no second anchor; he has not built one. This single dependency is the structural vulnerability of the entire system. Any handler, ally, or hostile party modeling Robbie must understand: Gabe is not *a* pressure point. Gabe is *the* pressure point, and almost every other pressure point reduces to him.

**Leverage.** Asymmetric and dangerous. Direct threats to Gabe trigger immediate, lethal-tier escalation — leverage that "works" in the sense of producing compliance only within a very narrow window before transitioning into uncontrolled retaliation. *Genuine help* to Gabe, by contrast, produces a near-permanent, inarticulate loyalty that Robbie himself cannot describe and may not consciously credit; this is the more durable form of access. Adversaries who threaten Gabe earn termination. Allies who treat Gabe as a person, not a complication, earn a brother — which Robbie has, until this point, only ever been to one person.

---

### 6.2 — Juan Reyes (father; absent, abusive, paternal template-to-refuse)

**Status.** Out of the home. Whereabouts variable across canon; for file purposes treat as present-but-absent — a man whose continued existence matters less than the imprint he left.

**History.** Juan Reyes (in the Smith run and subsequent recontextualizations) is established as the abusive father whose household Robbie and Gabe survived. The specific incidents are largely off-panel; canon is more interested in the *residue* than the catalog. The residue is visible everywhere: Robbie's flinch responses to raised hands, the way he positions his body between Gabe and any sudden noise, the practiced manner in which he absorbs verbal aggression without escalation, the deeply set aversion to alcohol in a culture and neighborhood where its use is unremarkable. Juan is also the conduit to Eli — biologically, the link by which the great-uncle's bloodline reaches Robbie. This is not coincidence in the narrative grammar of the property; it is the literalization of the *bad inheritance.*

**Dynamics.** There is no current dynamic. There is only the *anti-dynamic* — Robbie's entire adult character is organized as the photographic negative of his father. Where Juan drank, Robbie does not. Where Juan struck, Robbie restrains his hands compulsively (the abstention from killing is partly Eli-resistance, partly Juan-resistance; the two motives are entangled). Where Juan abandoned, Robbie cannot leave. Where Juan was unpredictable, Robbie is metronomic in his routines around Gabe. The father is the silhouette Robbie spends every day not stepping into.

**Trust / safety vector.** Zero trust. The father is, in Robbie's somatic memory, a category of threat, not a person. If Juan returned and asked for entry into Gabe's life, the assessment is that Robbie would refuse, calmly, with violence held in reserve. He would not negotiate, would not seek reconciliation, would not invite an explanation. The door would not open. The hellfire would not necessarily ignite — but it would be available.

**Evolution.** Juan barely *appears*; his evolution is in Robbie's relationship to his memory. Early in the 2014 run, the father is a fresh wound — Robbie's avoidance is taut, reactive. By later volumes, the father has become more like geology: shaped Robbie permanently, no longer actively eroding. The danger is not that Robbie will be hurt by him again; the danger is that Robbie will *become* him, which Robbie monitors constantly and which is one of the two primary functions of his rigid self-discipline. (The other function is Eli-suppression.)

**Failure mode.** Two scenarios. (1) If Juan returned and threatened Gabe — or merely menaced Gabe in Robbie's perception — Robbie would kill him, with or without Eli's prompting, and the moral architecture that prevents him from killing other men would not engage, because the father is the one figure exempted from Robbie's universalist refusal-to-kill. This is the single, narrow opening in his ethics, and Robbie probably does not know it is there until the test arrives. (2) If, conversely, Robbie were ever to discover *himself* having struck Gabe in anger or having parented through fear, the resulting shame-collapse would be catastrophic and possibly unrecoverable; he would read it as having become his father, and the verdict would be self-sentencing.

**Leverage.** Limited and dirty. Anyone weaponizing Juan against Robbie — using the father as a tool of leverage, blackmail, or psychological destabilization — would produce intense aversive reaction but also an unusually clear, controlled response, because Robbie has spent years *thinking about* his father. The father is a closed file in his mind; threats routed through him are predictable. The genuinely effective leverage points are Gabe and Eli, not Juan. Juan is the wound. Gabe is the heart.

---

### 6.3 — The Mother (deceased / absent; the unspoken)

**Status.** Most consistent canon read: dead. In some treatments simply gone. Either way, functionally absent across the entire published life of the subject.

**History.** The mother is the *least* documented figure in the Reyes family and, by inverse correlation, possibly the most influential silence in the file. Robbie does not speak about her. Photographs are not catalogued. Anniversaries are not observed on-panel. She is referenced glancingly, occasionally, and in a register that approaches reverence — the way one would say a prayer rather than tell a story. This is consistent with two possible readings, and the file holds both open: (a) she died when Robbie was old enough to remember her clearly and the grief is still organized; (b) she died or left when he was young enough that she has become an *idea* rather than a memory, around which he has assembled a private and probably idealized image.

**Dynamics — by absence.** Her absence is the original parentification event. The moment she was no longer present to mother Gabe, the role transferred — not to Juan, who was incapable or unwilling, but to Robbie, who took it and has not put it down. Every meal Robbie cooks, every bath he draws, every wheelchair he pushes is in some functional sense a mother's labor performed by a son. He has not, across published canon, articulated this aloud. He almost certainly has not articulated it to himself. It is one of the most important unspoken facts of his life.

**Trust / safety vector.** Idealized. The dead mother is the only family figure who is *safe* in Robbie's interior landscape — safe because she cannot disappoint, cannot strike, cannot demand. She is the warmth at the back of the house in his memory. Whether the real woman matched the memory is not something Robbie has interrogated, and likely will not.

**Evolution.** Static. The mother does not change because she is not present to change. Her role in his psychology is to function as a quiet, fixed reference point — the proof that there *was* once a home, that the household was not always a war zone, that tenderness is not foreign to the Reyes name. This is structurally important: without that reference point, Robbie's tenderness toward Gabe would have no precedent in his own life, and tenderness without precedent is hard to sustain. She is, in this sense, the silent precondition for Robbie's caregiving capacity.

**Failure mode.** Discovery that the idealized image is wrong — that she abandoned them, that she was complicit in the father's violence, that she chose her own departure — would destabilize one of the few uncompromised structures in his psychology. He has not stress-tested this image. The file flags it as a latent vulnerability rather than an active one.

**Leverage.** Negligible direct. High indirect via the *category* of mother-figures: women who occupy maternal roles in Robbie's environment (older female neighbors, certain Avengers, the rare nurturing authority figure) trigger a small but consistent softening in his guard. He is not aware of this. It is, in operational terms, the only soft spot in his interpersonal defenses that does not route through Gabe.

---

### 6.4 — Eli Morrow (great-uncle; dead; resident parasite; second father)

**Status.** Deceased in body; resident in Robbie's skull as a discrete possessing consciousness, partially fused with a Spirit of Vengeance through occult homicide. Functionally: a dead man, a ghost, a parasite, a coach, a saboteur, and — whether either party would name it — a *family elder.*

**History.** Eli was a serial killer, a Satanist, and the original murderer whose blood-rite bound his soul to the 1969 Dodge Charger that Robbie later steals and dies in. His relationship to Robbie predates Robbie's awareness of it: the bloodline carries him, the car carries him, the family history carries him. When Robbie is shot, Eli is *waiting* in the vehicle, and the bonding is not selected by either party in any meaningful sense — Eli takes the host available; Robbie takes the resurrection available. Neither consents to the other. Both are, in this respect, victims of the arrangement, though Eli's victimhood is considerably attenuated by his pre-existing moral catalog.

**Dynamics.** This is the most psychologically complicated relationship in the file. Eli speaks to Robbie continuously, contextually, with a coherent personality — coaxing, mocking, advising, threatening, occasionally helpful. The Eli voice is recognizable across writers: dry, older, contemptuous of weakness, fluent in violence, paternal in a corrupted register. He calls Robbie "kid" and "mijo" interchangeably, and the second word matters: Eli has assumed the *uncle* register and is performing avuncular intimacy as a vector for influence. Robbie has — twice over — been raised by men he should not have trusted: a violent father in his childhood, and now a murdering ghost in his skull. The pattern is grimly consistent.

The negotiation is daily. Eli pushes for lethal force; Robbie refuses; Eli pushes again; Robbie refuses; under extreme provocation (threat to Gabe, threat to children, exhaustion), the refusal slips. Eli's leverage grows in the slip and shrinks in the recovery. Robbie's mental life is partly a continuous bargaining session with a creature who is, by any honest accounting, the worst person Robbie knows — and who lives behind his own eyes.

**Trust / safety vector.** Inverted and stable. Robbie does not trust Eli at all and assumes hostile intent at every decision point. This is, paradoxically, the safest possible posture: an internal saboteur whose hostility is fully expected cannot easily ambush. The danger is not Eli's lies (Robbie discounts them) but Eli's *truths* — the moments when Eli is correct about a tactical situation, when his advice would work, when his cynicism predicts an ally's betrayal. Each of those moments is a small concession to Eli's authority, and the accumulation is what Robbie has to guard against.

**Evolution.** Across the Smith → Waid → Aaron → Cates → Percy progression, Eli's role mutates. In the earliest arcs he is straightforwardly antagonistic and frequently dominant in the possession transitions. Across later volumes a strained operational truce emerges — not friendship, never that, but a working arrangement under which Eli contributes power and tactical input in exchange for the ride and the occasional permitted indulgence (a kill Robbie can convince himself was justified). The relationship comes to resemble that of an addict and a substance: useful, dangerous, never trusted, never quite expelled, periodically renegotiated. Robbie has had real opportunities to be free of Eli and has not consistently taken them; the file reads this not as ambivalence about Eli but as ambivalence about the *power* — Robbie distrusts his own ability to protect Gabe without it. This is one of the few places where Robbie's stated values and his operational behavior diverge, and it is worth flagging.

**Failure mode.** Two distinct catastrophic scenarios. (1) Eli gains sustained dominance — for example, through a moment of acute grief or unconsciousness — and operates Robbie's body for an extended period during which Eli's catalog (murder, predation) becomes visible to Gabe or to civilians who can identify Robbie. The shame-fallout would likely fracture the containment system permanently. (2) Robbie attempts an exorcism or expulsion and succeeds, but loses the resurrection along with the parasite — i.e., the price of expelling Eli is dying again. The assessment is that Robbie would accept this trade if Gabe were independently safe; the assessment is also that he is unlikely ever to believe Gabe is independently safe.

**Leverage.** Eli is *leverage* on Robbie more than he is *leveraged*. Adversaries who understand the Eli dynamic can manipulate Robbie indirectly by forcing situations in which Eli's counsel becomes structurally correct — moral dilemmas where killing is the cleanest solution, time-pressure scenarios in which restraint costs lives. These situations corrode Robbie faster than direct combat. Conversely, anyone who can speak credibly to *Eli* — bargain with him, threaten him, contain him — gains a small lever on Robbie's stability, for better or worse. Very few characters in the published canon have this capacity; those who do (Hellstrom, Blaze in specific moods, certain mystic operators) are the closest things to genuine mentors Robbie has ever had, and he resists every one of them, because accepting their mentorship means acknowledging that what is inside him is real, permanent, and a family matter rather than a problem he can fix at the workbench.

---

### 6.5 — Synthesis: the four-body system

The Reyes family configuration is structurally unstable in a precise, diagnosable way. There is exactly one positive anchor (Gabe), one absent idealization (the mother), one repudiated template (the father), and one resident hostile elder (Eli). Three of the four relationships are with the dead, the absent, or the parasitic; only one is with a living, present person — and that person is a dependent child. The entire emotional weight of Robbie's life rests on a relationship that is, by developmental necessity, temporary in its current form. Gabe will grow. Gabe will, eventually, want independence. Gabe may, eventually, not need Robbie in the way Robbie needs to be needed.

No one in the published canon — including Robbie — has fully reckoned with what happens when that day arrives. The file flags it as the central long-horizon vulnerability of the subject. Every other family relationship is fixed (the dead do not change; the parasite changes only in its bargaining position; the father is a closed file). Only Gabe is a moving variable. The system is therefore organized around the one element guaranteed to transform — and the subject has built no redundancy. This is not a criticism; it is a description. The boy has been holding his entire interior life together with one hand on his brother's wheelchair for as long as the camera has been on him. The astonishing thing is not that the arrangement is fragile. The astonishing thing is that it has held.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: ROBERT "ROBBIE" REYES
## Section 9 — Relationships II: Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

Preliminary note. The subject's allied roster is small by superhero-population standards and deliberately so. Robbie does not accumulate friends; he tolerates them in proximity to a task and lets the relationship lapse when the task ends. The dossier below catalogues every figure who has, at some point, crossed the threshold from "person Robbie works near" to "person Robbie would answer the phone for at 3 a.m." That threshold is high. The list is correspondingly short.

A second methodological note. With this subject, the *failure mode* of each relationship is more diagnostic than its high point. Robbie is most legible when a bond breaks or strains, because the break exposes the rules he has been silently enforcing all along. Each subsection therefore terminates in an explicit failure-mode and leverage analysis, in the file's standard format.

---

### 9.1 Gabriel "Gabe" Reyes — Younger Brother / Sole Primary Attachment

Listed under Allies for completeness but doubled in **Section 8 (Family)** as the primary attachment figure. The brief summary here is for cross-reference; the load-bearing analysis lives there. Gabe is not an ally in any conventional operational sense — he is the *axis* around which the entire ally schema is calibrated. Every other person on this list is graded, consciously or otherwise, against the single question: *does this relationship make Gabe safer, or more exposed?*

Operational consequence: any ally Robbie keeps for longer than one arc has, at some point, passed that test, whether or not they knew they were taking it.

---

### 9.2 Canelo Calaveras — Employer, Surrogate Father, Earliest Mentor

**History.** Canelo owns and runs Canelo's Auto and Body, the East L.A. garage where Robbie is employed pre-resurrection. The relationship predates the events of *All-New Ghost Rider* #1 by an unspecified but substantial interval — Robbie is already trusted with keys, with after-hours access, with customer cars, and with the working rhythms of the shop. This is not a Saturday job; it is an apprenticeship-grade placement of a minor who is clearly being kept on the payroll partly out of charity and partly because he is good at it.

**Dynamics.** Canelo is the closest thing Robbie has to a non-abusive adult male model. The relationship is procedural rather than emotional: they communicate through engines, schedules, and small mutual courtesies. Canelo does not ask about the home situation, but it is clear he knows the broad outlines (the absent father, the disabled brother, the food-insecurity), and the assessment is that he calibrates Robbie's hours, his pay, and his autonomy accordingly. Robbie does not name this as kindness, but he accepts it — one of the very few instances on record of Robbie accepting structural assistance without recoil.

**Evolution.** Canelo is murdered (All-New Ghost Rider #4) by Grumpy's men in the early arc — a casualty of the Hyde / Calvin Zabo conflict that has been pulled into the neighborhood by Robbie's involvement. This death is one of the foundational guilt-events of the post-resurrection psyche. Canelo died because Robbie's secondary world reached into Canelo's primary one. The subject does not articulate this guilt in dialogue, but the behavioral signature is immediate and durable: post-Canelo, Robbie's *concealment doctrine* hardens into operational law. Anyone close enough to be collateral becomes, by definition, someone who must not know.

**Trust / safety.** While alive: among the highest of any non-Gabe figure. Canelo represented a stable, low-demand, competence-respecting adult presence — exactly the relational template Robbie's developmental history had withheld. Post-mortem, Canelo functions as a posthumous internalized voice, the residual model for what a *good adult man* sounds like at a workbench. The garage itself becomes a memorial site.

**Failure mode.** The relationship failed by *external lethal interruption*, not by relational breakdown. This is critical: Robbie never got the chance to disappoint Canelo, never got to lose him to misunderstanding. The bond is therefore idealized in memory, undamaged by the corrosive secrecy that erodes all of Robbie's *living* relationships. Canelo's death also instantiated Robbie's defining belief — *I am dangerous to those I love* — in a real, observable, blood-on-the-floor instance.

**Leverage profile.** Posthumous. Canelo's memory can be invoked by anyone who knew him to produce compliance, sobriety, and re-anchoring in Robbie. Canelo's name spoken by a stranger is, conversely, an immediate threat flag. The garage as physical space carries Canelo's residue; depriving Robbie of access to that space is an underestimated destabilizer.

---

### 9.3 Eli Morrow — The Inhabited Antagonist Who Functions Structurally as a Mentor-Inversion

A note on placement. Eli is catalogued in adversary files and in family files. He appears here, briefly, because the relationship has — episodically, especially in cosmic-tier arcs — assumed an *operational alliance* shape. When external threat exceeds Robbie's solo capacity, Eli's tactical knowledge and his willingness to escalate become a usable resource; Robbie has, on the record, leaned into Eli's counsel during the King-of-Hell and One-Below-All sequences.

The full analysis lives in the adversary file. The note required here is this: *Eli is the only "ally" Robbie cannot conceal from, cannot leave, and cannot stop hearing.* The relationship has the topology of an arranged marriage between a hostage and a captor inside one skull. It is the most intimate relationship in the subject's life by sheer hours of co-presence, and it is the one that produces all of the others' constraints. Every ally Robbie shields from his Rider identity is being shielded specifically from Eli, not from the hellfire.

---

### 9.4 Johnny Blaze — Predecessor, Reluctant Senior, Functional Half-Mentor

**History.** Blaze enters Robbie's orbit in the second half of the Smith run and recurs across the *Damnation* event, the Cates run, and the Percy solo. The encounter framework is consistent: Blaze appears when the supernatural ledger requires another Rider's intervention, exchanges minimal information, performs the function, and departs. The relationship is never sustained between flare-ups.

**Dynamics.** Blaze recognizes Robbie immediately as a structural sibling — same condition, different etiology. The mode is laconic: two men who have both been forcibly merged with infernal entities have less to explain to each other than to anyone else, and they spend the gained time mostly in silence. Blaze's senior status is acknowledged by Robbie behaviorally — he listens more than he talks in Blaze's presence, a rare configuration — but Blaze does not exploit this and does not attempt to formally mentor. The teaching, where it happens, is by demonstration: how to ride harder, how to take a hit from a demonic-tier opponent, what the Stare costs.

**Evolution.** Across the recurrences, a slow asymmetry has emerged. Blaze, in the Cates run, becomes briefly King of Hell. Robbie, later, occupies the same throne and abdicates it. The two arcs are essentially a single shared lesson conducted in series: *the throne does not solve anything, and the throne wants to keep you.* Blaze functions as a cautionary precedent more than a guide; the file's read is that Robbie has internalized Blaze's mistakes as warnings about the cost of letting the Rider's mythology eat the man's life.

**Trust / safety.** Operationally high, relationally low. Robbie trusts Blaze in a firefight against demonic threat absolutely. Robbie does not trust Blaze with Gabe, with the garage's location, or with personal disclosure. Blaze has never asked.

**Failure mode.** The relationship fails by *attrition and non-cultivation.* Neither man calls the other socially. The bond renews only when a sufficiently large infernal event forces it to. If Blaze were to die or be permanently incapacitated, Robbie's grief would be real and largely unspoken; the practical loss would be the disappearance of the only person on Earth who fully understood the condition without explanation. The risk of *positive* failure — i.e., Blaze pulling Robbie too far into the mythic-Rider identity track — is contained by Robbie's own resistance to that frame and Blaze's apparent willingness to let him resist.

**Leverage profile.** Anyone using Blaze against Robbie would have to offer Blaze in genuine, mortal danger from a demonic actor to mobilize him. Blaze as bait works once. Blaze as moral authority telling Robbie to do something Robbie does not want to do — limited efficacy; Robbie respects Blaze's existence but does not accept his counsel as binding.

---

### 9.5 Daniel "Danny" Ketch — Peer-Tier Rider, Conditional Co-Belligerent

**History.** Ketch appears in the broader Ghost Rider material as the second classical Rider, and his interactions with Robbie are concentrated in the Cates and Percy runs and in event crossovers.

**Dynamics.** Where Blaze is the silent older brother, Ketch is the unstable cousin. Robbie's behavioral signature with Ketch is *guarded distance*. Ketch is theatrical where Robbie is mute, mystically literate where Robbie is mechanically literate, and prone to embracing the Rider identity in ways that Robbie reads as a warning rather than a model. Robbie cooperates with Ketch when forced and does not seek him out otherwise.

**Trust / safety.** Moderate-to-low. Robbie believes Ketch will show up; he is not certain which Ketch will arrive. Ketch's instability around Rider identity is exactly the failure mode Robbie organizes his own existence to avoid.

**Failure mode.** Identity contagion. Sustained proximity to Ketch increases the risk that Robbie's quiet, anti-mythic posture is eroded by repeated exposure to a Rider whose self-concept *is* the Rider. Robbie manages this by short engagements only.

**Leverage profile.** Ketch is not effective as bait — Robbie's investment is too thin. Ketch is, however, usable as a *frame-shifter*: place Ketch and Robbie in the same operational theater long enough and Robbie's resistance to mythic Rider framing weakens by simple comparative arithmetic.

---

### 9.6 Daimon Hellstrom — Occult Consultant, Held-at-Arm's-Length Asset

**History.** Hellstrom enters Robbie's network during the *Damnation* event and intermittently thereafter when the operational problem is specifically infernal-bureaucratic rather than violent.

**Dynamics.** Hellstrom is, in Robbie's classification, *useful but not trusted.* He provides expertise — naming of entities, mapping of hellish geographies, ritual scaffolding — that Robbie cannot get elsewhere. Robbie listens, executes, and leaves. He does not engage with Hellstrom's heritage as a peer condition, despite the structural parallel (both men carrying an infernal inheritance not of their choosing). Robbie's read of Hellstrom is that the man has *negotiated* with his condition where Robbie has only *contained* his; that negotiation is morally suspect to Robbie's working-class refusal-of-complicity ethic.

**Trust / safety.** Low and stable. Robbie does not bring Hellstrom near Gabe under any circumstance and will execute the consult in neutral territory.

**Failure mode.** Hellstrom's perpetual brokering posture — every transaction in his world has terms — fundamentally clashes with Robbie's all-or-nothing moral grammar. The relationship would fail catastrophically if Hellstrom ever attempted to charge Robbie for service in a coin Robbie regards as obscene (a soul, a child, a kill).

**Leverage profile.** Hellstrom cannot leverage Robbie. Robbie can leverage Hellstrom only in the narrow band of mutual infernal interest.

---

### 9.7 The Avengers, Aggregate — Institutional Affiliation Held as Loose Confederation

**History.** Robbie is drafted into the Avengers during the Waid run (*Avengers* 2016–17), continues across the Aaron run, and remains nominally affiliated through subsequent reshufflings. He is the youngest active member at induction and the only one without a public identity, a costume in the conventional sense, or a press apparatus.

**Dynamics.** Robbie's posture toward the Avengers as institution is *cooperative non-participation.* He executes missions, sits silently in briefings, declines social invitations, and leaves the moment the operational reason for his presence ends. Vision and Wonder Man, in early debriefs, registered him as withholding; the assessment concurs but reframes: it is not withholding, it is *protective concealment* operating at organizational scale. The Avengers are, from his working-class East L.A. vantage, a high-surveillance institution — exactly the kind he has spent his life evading.

**Trust / safety.** Robbie trusts the Avengers in combat. He does not trust the Avengers with his address, with Gabe's existence as anything more than a vaguely named dependent, with the specifics of his condition, or with discretionary authority over him. He has not, on record, given any teammate his phone number for non-mission use.

**Failure mode.** Institutional capture. If the Avengers as institution ever attempted to *codify* Robbie — assign a costume designer, schedule press, formalize a chain of command, brand him — he would disengage, possibly violently. The relationship survives only by remaining informal, episodic, and field-only.

**Leverage profile.** The Avengers as collective have minimal leverage. Individual Avengers who respect Robbie's terms have substantial situational leverage on a per-mission basis.

The individuals who matter inside that aggregate are treated below.

---

### 9.8 The Vision — The Avenger Who Read Him Most Accurately

**Dynamics.** Vision's analytic faculty produced, in early Avengers debriefs, the most accurate institutional read of Robbie on record. Robbie noticed. The relationship that resulted is unusual: Robbie tolerates Vision's observation in a way he tolerates no one else's, because Vision does not pretend the observation is anything other than what it is. There is no false intimacy.

**Trust / safety.** Moderate; the highest within the Avengers institutionally. Robbie has accepted Vision's tactical instruction on multiple occasions without the resistance he shows toward Tony Stark / Steve Rogers-tier authority. The dossier reads this as Vision's *non-mammalian register* removing the paternal-authority threat-signal that Robbie's nervous system flags around male elders.

**Failure mode.** Vision's analytic frankness, deployed without warning in front of Gabe or in front of a vulnerable third party, would breach Robbie's concealment doctrine and end the relationship. Vision has not done this. The risk is low, but it is the failure mode.

**Leverage profile.** Vision can give Robbie an instruction in the field and be obeyed. Vision cannot socialize him.

---

### 9.9 Wonder Man, Captain America (Rogers), Thor, et al. — The Senior Avengers as a Class

**Dynamics.** Robbie's posture toward the senior Avengers is uniform: respectful, compliant on mission, socially absent. He addresses Rogers as "Captain" and not "Steve," does not joke, does not eat with the team. Thor and Hercules's drinking-and-feasting register is alien to him; he attends once, does not return.

**Trust / safety.** Operational only. None of these figures have ever met Gabe.

**Failure mode.** Mentorship attempts. If any senior Avenger has tried to take Robbie under wing in the conventional sense — sit-down talks, professional development, ideological transmission — the record indicates Robbie absorbs perhaps one such interaction and then evades subsequent ones. Captain America's particular failure pattern, the assessment infers, is that his offered framework (duty, country, the larger calling) lands wrong: Robbie's duty is to one wheelchair-bound person on one block, and any framework that scales upward feels like an invitation to abandon that post.

**Leverage profile.** Minimal. These figures cannot move Robbie via personal relationship and must move him, if at all, via operational logic and threat-to-innocents framing.

---

### 9.10 The Younger Avengers Cohort (Kamala Khan, Miles Morales, Nova / Sam Alexander, Amadeus Cho, when overlapping) — The Peer Tier He Should Have Bonded With and Mostly Didn't

**Dynamics.** On paper this is Robbie's natural cohort: teenagers in over their heads, juggling secret identities with school and family obligation, sharing class and ethnic markers in several cases. The bonds did not consolidate. The dossier's read: the *peer condition* exposes Robbie's parentification too clearly. He cannot relate to teenagers who are *also being kids*; he stopped being a kid when he became Gabe's caretaker. Their lightness reads to him as a different species of life. He likes them. He envies them faintly. He does not socialize with them.

There are partial exceptions on the record — brief warmth with Kamala Khan when a mission turned on protecting a disabled bystander, a flash of mutual recognition with Miles Morales over family-secret management — but neither bond was cultivated.

**Trust / safety.** Operational-positive, social-neutral.

**Failure mode.** Non-cultivation. The relationships fail by his failure to call them back.

**Leverage profile.** Threats to any of these figures in his presence will mobilize him to a high degree, especially Kamala (the disability-adjacent reflex) and any of them when configured as *kid who shouldn't be in this fight.*

---

### 9.11 Doctor Strange — Reluctantly Accepted Occult Authority

**History.** Strange enters the field most directly during *Damnation* and recurs as the senior occult voice in events that touch Robbie's domain.

**Dynamics.** Robbie's posture toward Strange is the rarest configuration in his catalogue: *grudging deference to expertise without affection.* He recognizes Strange's competence in the metaphysical register where his own expertise is mechanical, and he accepts instruction in that narrow lane. Outside it, he does not engage. Strange has, the record suggests, taken Robbie seriously as an operational adult rather than patronizing him — and Robbie has noticed and reciprocated within the lane.

**Trust / safety.** Lane-specific.

**Failure mode.** Strange's habit of arranging large metaphysical bargains on others' behalf would terminate the relationship if it ever extended to Robbie's soul, the Rider's binding, or — worst case — anything involving Gabe.

**Leverage profile.** Limited; bidirectional and respectful within domain.

---

### 9.12 The One Below All / Cosmic-Tier Antagonist-Allies — Catalogued Only as Confirmation

Cross-listed primarily in the adversary file. Mentioned here because in the Aaron *Avengers* run and Cates *Ghost Rider* run Robbie has been forced into provisional alliance with entities that should be enemies. The pattern is consistent: Robbie will negotiate with anything for one mission if the alternative is Gabe / innocents harmed, and he will disengage at the first lawful opportunity afterward. He does not accumulate cosmic friends.

---

### 9.13 Gabe's Caregivers, School Aides, and Neighborhood Adults — The Quiet Civilian Network

**Dynamics.** A subdossier of low-visibility individuals that the assessment infers must exist around Gabe's care: a school aide, a clinic intake nurse, possibly a neighbor woman who watches Gabe during late shifts, a special-education teacher, a regular pharmacist. Few of these figures appear on-panel; their existence is logistically required by Gabe's care profile and Robbie's work hours.

**Trust / safety.** Compartmented and earned in small increments. Robbie's working assumption with each is that they are decent until proven otherwise; he tests them by leaving Gabe with them for short, escalating intervals; he locks the relationship in once Gabe responds warmly to them.

**Failure mode.** Single-incident. Any betrayal of Gabe's safety — a missed pickup, an unreported injury, a hand laid wrongly — terminates the relationship instantly and forever. There is no second chance in this layer.

**Leverage profile.** Anyone who has been quietly kind to Gabe over time has earned a stratum of Robbie's loyalty that he himself cannot fully account for. Conversely, anyone in this layer who is identified by an adversary becomes an immediate high-value bait target — the assessment flags this as the *most under-defended attack surface* in the subject's life. He compartments his super-life from his civilian life rigorously; he has not been forced, yet, to consider that an adversary might attack from the civilian side.

---

### 9.14 The Charger — Catalogued Here Despite Object Status

The 1969 Dodge Charger is treated by the subject as an ally, and the file follows his usage. The vehicle is the physical interface to Eli, the carrier of Canelo's last lessons (it was rebuilt in Canelo's garage), and the only entity that has been continuously with Robbie from pre-resurrection to current operations. He talks to it. He maintains it ritually (Section 11 — Habits & Rituals catalogues this in detail). He grieves damage to it disproportionately.

**Trust / safety.** Total. The Charger has never betrayed him; Eli's betrayals are catalogued as Eli's, not the car's.

**Failure mode.** Destruction of the Charger would precipitate a containment crisis with Eli of unknown magnitude. The vehicle is the somatic anchor.

**Leverage profile.** Anyone who damages the Charger has committed, in Robbie's affective ledger, an act adjacent to assaulting Gabe. The dossier's recommendation, for any party modeling him: do not touch the car.

---

### 9.15 Synthesis — The Shape of the Allied Network

Five observations close this section.

**First.** The network is *radial, not reticular.* Every ally connects to Robbie; almost none connect to each other. He has built no team in the conventional sense. The Avengers are his nominal team and they do not know his brother's last name.

**Second.** The network is *crisis-activated.* Most of these relationships do not exist between flare-ups. Robbie has no friends he calls weekly. The relational baseline is silence; the relational maximum is shoulder-to-shoulder in a fight, then silence again.

**Third.** The network is *under-defended at the civilian layer.* Robbie has hardened his super-life against penetration and left his civilian life — Gabe's aides, the neighborhood adults, the pharmacist, the school — relatively exposed because he is still operating on the assumption that adversaries fight on super-terrain. The assessment flags this as his greatest unaddressed vulnerability.

**Fourth.** The network is *anchored by the dead.* Canelo, posthumously, organizes Robbie's image of the kind of


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# UATU ANALYTICAL FILE: ROBERT "ROBBIE" REYES
## Section: Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

---

A note before the entries. The subject's enemy ledger is, in a literal sense, longer than his friend ledger and much longer than his lover ledger — the last being, by any honest reading of canon, effectively empty. This is itself the assessment's most important relational datum. A nineteen-to-early-twenties male with the physiology, the proximity to civilian populations, and the cultural baseline of East Los Angeles, who has logged exactly zero documented sexual or romantic relationships across nearly a decade of in-universe and publication time, is communicating something about himself. The empty lover-column is not a writers' oversight; it is consistent across five different creative regimes (Smith, Waid, Aaron, Cates, Percy). The subject does not date. The subject does not flirt. The subject does not, on the record, register attraction. This will be addressed in its own sub-section at the close.

The enemy ledger, by contrast, is structured. It sorts into four strata: (a) neighborhood-scale predators who initiated the trauma (Hyde, Grumpy, the Catrinas); (b) the parasitic intimate enemy who lives inside his skull (Eli Morrow); (c) cosmic-tier adversaries inherited by virtue of the Rider mantle (Mephisto, Zadkiel-era leftovers, the One Below All, Lilith, King-of-Hell competitors); and (d) intra-Rider rivals and frenemies (Blaze, Ketch, Hellstrom, briefly Kushala) who function less as enemies than as comparative templates the subject refuses to become. Each is mapped below at the granularity a total-surveillance file would demand.

---

### 1. ELI MORROW — the intimate enemy, the resident parasite, the second father

**History.** The great-uncle, retconned from the originally-introduced serial-killer-ghost-in-the-car. A Satanist and confirmed multiple murderer in life, bound to the Charger through occult homicide and a deal with infernal powers; later canon merges his bound ghost with a genuine Spirit of Vengeance, so that what speaks in the subject's skull is *both* a specific dead man with a name and a face and a cosmic engine of damnation that has absorbed him. The subject did not know Eli existed before the resurrection. Eli has known the subject since infancy from the family-historical archive of the dead — knew his name, knew Gabe's name, knew the abusive father's name, and exploited every one of those data points within hours of becoming audible.

**Dynamics.** The relationship is conducted as a continuous, low-volume interior negotiation that periodically escalates to crisis. Eli is not a hallucination and not an alter; he is a discrete agent with his own goals (autonomy, embodiment, the resumption of the killing he was doing in life, eventual escape into a body the subject does not control). The subject's posture toward him is the posture of a sober man living with an active drinker: never leave the keys out, never agree, never argue past the point of fatigue, never accept the small favor that becomes the leash. Eli's primary tactic is the reasonable-sounding suggestion at the threshold of crisis — *kill him, you know you want to, he hurt Gabe, you'll feel better, you'll sleep* — calibrated precisely to the subject's most loaded affect. Eli is, in a clinical sense, an expert on the subject. He has surveillance access to thought, memory, and somatic state that no human adversary could replicate.

**Evolution.** Early (Smith run): overt psychomachy, Eli openly antagonistic, the subject openly disgusted. Middle (Gabe-possession arc, vol. 1 finale): Eli demonstrates he can body-jump, weaponize loved ones, and force the subject into no-win moral architectures. Subject's response — reabsorb the parasite into himself rather than kill the host — sets the template for every subsequent Eli encounter and is the load-bearing behavior of the entire dossier. Later (Damnation, Cates, Percy): Eli becomes occasionally tactically useful, contributing infernal lore and combat instincts; the subject accepts the contributions, refuses the friendship, treats every offered tool as potentially booby-trapped. By the most recent issues, the relationship has stabilized into something resembling a forced and bitter marriage of incompatibles: cohabitation without intimacy, cooperation without trust, neither able to evict the other.

**Trust / safety.** Zero trust, asymptotic. The subject's safety protocol with Eli is essentially: assume the worst interpretation of every offered insight; check Eli's claims against external evidence whenever possible; never make a major moral decision while transformed; if the decision must be made while transformed, default to *the minimum violence that protects Gabe*. Safety is maintained through procedural rigidity, not through any softening of the relationship.

**Failure mode.** Eli wins when (i) the subject is exhausted, sleep-deprived, or unconscious — the standard human vulnerability windows; (ii) Gabe is in mortal danger and the only available solution is a moral compromise the subject would normally refuse; (iii) the subject is isolated from the garage and from Gabe long enough that his organizing identity-anchors weaken. The Pleasant Hill stretch, the King-of-Hell stretch, and any prolonged cosmic deployment all degrade his resistance. The catastrophic failure mode — not yet realized in canon, but mapped by the assessment — is loss of Gabe. With Gabe gone, the leash on Eli is gone, and the question of who is driving the body becomes open.

**Leverage.** Eli's leverage on the subject is Gabe and the threat of becoming-the-father. The subject's leverage on Eli is the body itself — Eli wants out, wants embodiment, wants the kill, and cannot have any of it without the subject's cooperation or unconsciousness. The subject withholds. It is the longest, quietest negotiation in the file.

---

### 2. CALVIN ZABO / MISTER HYDE — the originating murderer

**History.** The pharmaceutical-criminal whose stolen contraband was in the trunk of the Charger; Hyde's enforcers executed the subject on Hyde's order. Hyde is, in a strict sense, the man who killed him. The subject was eighteen, unarmed, on a side street, lit by headlights.

**Dynamics.** The subject's relationship to Hyde is unusual in a revenge-genre context: it is *not* organized around revenge. The first arc (issues #1–5) has him confront Hyde, defeat him, and decline to kill him. The decision is not mercy in the ethical sense; it is identity-protection. Killing Hyde would, in the subject's interior logic, ratify Eli's reading of him as a murderer-in-waiting and would constitute an act Gabe could not be told about. He chooses the burden of leaving Hyde alive over the relief of resolving him.

**Evolution.** Hyde recedes from the active file after the opening arc. He is not pursued, not hunted, not used as a recurring trophy adversary. The subject does not collect Hyde the way Blaze collects his enemies. Hyde becomes, behaviorally, a closed file — the originating wound, processed once, filed, not revisited.

**Trust / safety.** N/A; pure adversary, but a passive one in the subject's psychology. The subject does not fear Hyde personally; he fears what killing Hyde would do to his self-concept.

**Failure mode.** The subject's refusal to finish Hyde leaves a permanent open vector — Hyde lives, Hyde could come back, Hyde knows what the Charger contained and what the subject became. The subject has accepted this risk because the alternative violates the prime directive of his personality.

**Leverage.** Hyde has none on the subject post-arc. The subject has none on Hyde he is willing to use.

---

### 3. "GRUMPY" / NEIGHBORHOOD CATRINA LEADERSHIP — the local predators

**History.** The Catrinas are the East L.A. gang operating in the subject's blocks; Grumpy is the lieutenant who repeatedly menaces Gabe and the household. They predate the resurrection; they are the everyday threat-floor of the subject's life since middle school.

**Dynamics.** Pre-Rider, the subject managed them through avoidance, route-planning, and the small-scale diplomacy of a kid who knew which corner not to walk past at which hour. Post-Rider, the management shifts: he can now make them stop, but he refuses to escalate to lethal force, instead delivering measured terror — the burn-but-not-kill, the Penance-Stare-analog, the visible demonstration that he is no longer prey. The Catrinas function as the *practice ground* for his ethical containment of the Rider: every Catrina encounter is a calibration test for how much violence is enough.

**Evolution.** Across the runs, the Catrinas as an organization degrade in their threat profile to him personally while remaining a generalized threat to the neighborhood.
